<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="bc59fa0db0e0402ab2e498923356ad12"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="bc59fa0db0e0402ab2e498923356ad12">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '8418c3ec-27af-4c7c-be04-a3ab9d2307f6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d198112950" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Gels and batteries</b>: The “double-helix” structure in carrageenan–metal hydrogel was used as a new precursor for making the multiple metal sulfides/carbon aerogels with ultra-small nanoparticles and hierarchical porous structure. These aerogels exhibit high reversible capacity, excellent rate ability, and cycling stability for sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/af37c8c4-ab70-4fc3-8452-81312d66884f/anie201610301-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/af37c8c4-ab70-4fc3-8452-81312d66884f/anie201610301-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3744c76d-e976-4ac9-84be-6bfaad3bf5d9/anie201610301-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/af37c8c4-ab70-4fc3-8452-81312d66884f/anie201610301-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d198112953" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The metal sulfide-carbon nanocomposite is a new class of anode material for sodium ion batteries, but its development is restricted by its relative poor rate ability and cyclic stability. Herein, we report the use of double-helix structure of carrageenan–metal hydrogels for the synthesis of 3D metal sulfide (M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>) nanostructure/carbon aerogels (CAs) for high-performance sodium-ion storage. The method is unique, and can be used to make multiple M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs (such as FeS/CA, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/CA, Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CA, CuS/CA, ZnS/CA, and CdS/CA) with ultra-small nanoparticles and hierarchical porous structure by pyrolyzing the carrageenan–metal hydrogels. The as-prepared FeS/CA exhibits a high reversible capacity and excellent cycling stability (280 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 200 cycles) and rate performance (222 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) when used as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries. The work shows the value of biomass-derived metal sulfide–carbon heterostuctures in sodium-ion storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201610301-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Increasing global demand for clean energy has greatly promoted the development of high-performance energy storage and conversion devices. Thus, metal sulfide (MS) nanostructures have been extensively studied and applied in energy storage and conversion owing to their excellent electronic properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0001_R_d198112942e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">1</a>-<a href="#anie201610301-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0003_R_d198112942e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett.">3</a></sup></span> The typical examples include MoS<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> applied as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> used as the electrocatalysts for hydrogen/oxygen evolution reaction, and CdS applied as photocatalyst for photocatalysis or photoelectrocatalysis.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0004_R_d198112942e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>-<a href="#anie201610301-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0008_R_d198112942e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> However, the existed MS nanostructures usually have poor electrochemical performance in energy-storage and conversion devices owing to their unstable structure and unsatisfactory electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0009_R_d198112942e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span> Constructing nanocomposites of MS nanostructures with carbonaceous materials is an effective strategy to address the disadvantages of MS nanostructures, which can shorten the charge transport and ion diffusion distance and prevent the destruction of MS structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0010_R_d198112942e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a>-<a href="#anie201610301-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0013_R_d198112942e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span> In particular, the next-generation of SIBs are believed to have the potential to replace LIBs, owing to the low cost and natural abundant sodium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0014_R_d198112942e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">14</a></sup></span> Ultra-small MS nanoparticles (NPs) encapsulated by carbon are common anode materials for SIBs with relatively high specific capacity and excellent rate performance, but still suffer from poor cycle life because of the inefficient trapping of dissolved polysulfides anions during the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0015_R_d198112942e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Carrageenans are water-soluble, anionic, and linear polymers extracted from red algae (such as achondrus, eucheuma, and gigartina), and extensively used in the food, pharmaceutical, and cosmetic industries. They consist of alternating disaccharide repeating sulfate groups units of 3-linked-β-<span class="smallCaps">d</span>-galactopyranose and 4-linked-α-<span class="smallCaps">d</span>-galactopyranose or 4-linked-3,6-anhydro-α-<span class="smallCaps">d</span>-galactopyranose. They can be divided into three types depending on the number and position of sulfate groups, including kappa (κ)-, iota (ι)-, and lambda (λ)-carrageenan. Both κ- and ι-carrageenan aqueous solution can undergo sol–gel transition upon cooling or the addition of cations (such as, K<sup>+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, and Fe<sup>3+</sup>) through the formation of “double-helix” structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0016_R_d198112942e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbohydr. Res.">16</a></sup></span> To be specific, the sulfate groups are on the exterior of the double-helix, and the cations are able to interact with sulfate groups through ionic forces to hold the different double helices.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0017_R_d198112942e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbohydr. Res.">17</a>, <a href="#anie201610301-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0018_R_d198112942e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Food Hydrocolloids">18</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the cations not only assist the formation of the “double-helix” structure, but also promote the aggregation of different double-helix structures to form junction zones, which enable long-range cross-linking to form the carrageenan-cation hydrogels (Figure S1 in the Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0019_R_d198112942e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Biomater. Sci. Polym. Ed.">19</a></sup></span> Therefore, such double-helix structures are unique, but the use of them for constructing new MS NPs/carbon composites to address the challenging issues of SIBs associated with capacity, rate ability, and cycling stability is still a challenge, and has never been reported.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report the use of the double-helix structure in carrageenan-metal hydrogels for making multiple hierarchical porous MS NPs/carbon aerogels (M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs), including FeS/CA, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/CA, Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CA, CuS/CA, ZnS/CA, and CdS/CA, through a unique large-scale sustainable biomass conversion strategy. Among the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs, the FeS/CA as the anode material for SIBs exhibits a high reversible capacity of 380 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> as well as excellent cycling stability (280 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 Ag<sup>−1</sup> over 200 cycles) and rate performance (222 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         
         <p>As illustrated in Scheme <a href="#anie201610301-fig-5001">1</a> a, the ι-carrageenan macromolecules exist in aqueous solution as random coils at a high temperature of 80 °C. When the ι-carrageenan solution was dipped into metal (M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup>) ethanol solution, the ι-carrageenan macromolecules gelate with M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> ions leading to the formation of carrageenan–M hydrogels (Scheme <a href="#anie201610301-fig-5001">1</a> b). In this step, the M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> cations promote the conversion of random coil carrageenan macromolecule chains into a double-helix structure and the aggregation of them. The M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> cations are capable of forming intra-molecular bridges between the sulfate groups of ι-carrageenan. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectra show the effect of the addition of M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> cations on the position of the S−O stretching band of ι-carrageenan. The M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> cations led to the lower wavenumbers shift of S−O stretching band, indicating that they are involved in gelation to sulfate groups (Figure S2).<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0020_R_d198112942e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Biomacromolecules">20</a></sup></span> After freeze drying and subsequent pyrolysis of the carrageenan–M hydrogels, the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs nanocomposites with hierarchical porous structure were formed (Scheme <a href="#anie201610301-fig-5001">1</a> c). The weight percentage of M<sup><i>n</i>+</sup> species in the carrageenan–M aerogels was measured to be about 17–26 wt. % by using thermogravimetric analysis (TGA; Figure S3).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201610301-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3dc4a39e-f271-4f5c-b931-9b95314bc23c/anie201610301-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3dc4a39e-f271-4f5c-b931-9b95314bc23c/anie201610301-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e93247b-a0ad-4c85-9470-4c1373400afe/anie201610301-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3dc4a39e-f271-4f5c-b931-9b95314bc23c/anie201610301-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201610301-fig-5001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201610301" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The synthesis of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs from a carrageenan with a special double-helix structure. See text for details.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) was used to monitor the crystal structures of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0001">1</a> a). The broad diffraction peaks appear at 2<i>θ</i>=25.0°, corresponding to the (002) diffraction plane of the graphite structure in the CA matrix. The XRD patterns also show the sharp diffraction peaks indexed to FeS (JCPD no. 65-9124), Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (JCPD no. 65-1765), Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPD no. 47-1739), CuS (JCPD no. 06-0464), ZnS (JCPD no. 75-1547), and CdS (JCPD no. 41-1049), respectively. The Raman spectra of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs show the intensity of <span class="smallCaps">D</span>-band/G-band ratios is in range of 0.79–0.82 (Figure S4), indicating the CA matrix has a highly graphitic structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0021_R_d198112942e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Inorg. Chem.">21</a></sup></span> The carbon contents of the obtained M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs were determined to be 30–40 wt. % (Figure S5). The morphology of the generated M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs were studied by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and high resolution TEM (HRTEM). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0001">1</a> b,c and Figure S6, all the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs have a 3D self-supporting macroporous network with a pore diameter of about 2–5 μm and thin walls of less than 100–300 nm. A large amount of mesopores (in the range ca. 4–10 nm) are observed on the surface of FeS/CA. The macropores and large mesopores are formed during the freeze-drying of the carrageenan–M hydrogels. The specific surface area and pore size distribution of the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs were investigated through the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method and Barret–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of all the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs show the typical type IV adsorption isotherm (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0001">1</a> d); the BET specific surface area (S<sub>BET</sub>) of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs are 300–450 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and the corresponding single-point total pore volume are in range of 0.25–0.45 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Table S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0022_R_d198112942e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Mater.">22</a></sup></span> The pore size distributions of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs calculated from the adsorption branch of the isotherms show that the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs own micro/mesoporous structure with the mesopores centered at about 2–4 nm (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0001">1</a> e). The micropore and small mesopore of the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs should be attributed to the thermal decomposition of carrageenan macromolecule, where the oxygen- and sulfur-containing groups (-OH, -COOH, and -SO<sub>4</sub>H) were converted into H<sub>2</sub>O, CO<sub>2</sub>, and SO<sub>2</sub>. Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0002">2</a> a shows the TEM images of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> NPs-carbon nanocomposites. The M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> NPs with diameter of 5–20 nm are uniformly embedded in the CA matrix. The HRTEM images (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0002">2</a> b) show that the lattice fringe spacings of the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> NPs are well matched with those of FeS, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, CuS, ZnS, and CdS, respectively. Furthermore, the amorphous carbon surrounding the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> NPs contains abundant micropores, being in good agreement with the BET results. The TEM-energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings were used to analyze the elemental compositions of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs. Apparently, the M (M=Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, Cd), C, and S elements are homogeneously distributed in all the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0002">2</a> c).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201610301-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/38c796b7-94c5-4ef3-a9ef-4f457ecba558/anie201610301-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/38c796b7-94c5-4ef3-a9ef-4f457ecba558/anie201610301-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e41bc9b8-578f-482a-8418-08afe97e18f7/anie201610301-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/38c796b7-94c5-4ef3-a9ef-4f457ecba558/anie201610301-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201610301-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201610301" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) XRD patterns of the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs. b),c) FESEM images of FeS/CA. d) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isothermals and e) pore size distributions of the M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201610301-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1dc405c2-ebec-42f8-aa40-f4a65c27c3ee/anie201610301-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1dc405c2-ebec-42f8-aa40-f4a65c27c3ee/anie201610301-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c5c9070-2351-44c2-b15d-556b42bf57a5/anie201610301-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1dc405c2-ebec-42f8-aa40-f4a65c27c3ee/anie201610301-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201610301-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201610301" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) TEM, b) HRTEM images, and c) the EDS mappings of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of the as-obtained FeS/CA (as a model) was evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanic charge/discharge in a voltage range of 0.01–3 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> a shows the CV curves of FeS/CA in the first five cycles. The first scan CV curve shows four reductive peaks at 0.01, 0.35, 0.82, and 1.15 V. Four oxidation peaks are found at 0.09, 1.38, 1.59, and 2.16 V. In the subsequent four charge/discharge process, the reductive peaks at 0.82 and 1.15 V shift to 0.78 and 0.93 V owing to the formation of solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) film on the electrode surface in the first cycle, which leads to irreversible capacity loss. The reductive/oxidation peaks at 0.01 and 0.09 V correspond to interference of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction to porous carbon. The reductive peak in 0.93 V is attributed to intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into FeS (FeS+<i>x</i> Na<sup>+</sup>+xe<sup>−</sup>→Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeS), and the peak at 0.35 V arises from the conversion reaction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeS into Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0023_R_d198112942e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span> The three oxidation peaks at 1.38, 1.59, and 2.16 V demonstrate conversion of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe to FeS.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0010_R_d198112942e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> The subsequent four CV curves show stable reductive/oxidation peaks, indicating the FeS/CA exhibits good stability toward sodiation/desodiation. Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> b shows the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of FeS/CA anode at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. For the initial discharge profile, the plateaus at around 1.1, 0.8, and 0.3 V are inconsistent with the CV curve, corresponding to the conversion reaction of FeS to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe. The plateaus of subsequent discharge processes move to about 0.9 and 0.35 V. For the second and third cycles, the discharge/charge profiles almost overlap, indicating an outstanding reversibility of FeS/CA. The charge/discharge profiles also reveal the first discharge and charge specific capacities of FeS/CA are 780 and 439 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 56.3 %. The relatively low CE should be attributed to the formation of SEI on the surface of FeS/CA, the decomposition of the electrolyte and the irreversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0024_R_d198112942e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a>, <a href="#anie201610301-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0025_R_d198112942e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201610301-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3fcdabd8-56a5-4d81-a716-ce3ebc33ac53/anie201610301-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3fcdabd8-56a5-4d81-a716-ce3ebc33ac53/anie201610301-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8f0430e7-e640-4792-b914-26c012372bb1/anie201610301-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3fcdabd8-56a5-4d81-a716-ce3ebc33ac53/anie201610301-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201610301-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201610301" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) CV curves of FeS/CA in the first five cycles. b) The FeS/CA cycled at the 1st, 2nd, and 3rd between 0.01 and 3.0 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The specific capacity of the FeS/CA at different current densities. d) The specific capacity and cycling performance of FeS/CA at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) TEM and HRTEM images of FeS/CA anode after 200 cycles (charged to 3.0 V). f) Schematic illustration of how the FeS/CA in enhances SIBs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>We further explore the high-rate performance of the FeS/CA electrode at various current densities. The specific capacities of FeS/CA are 439, 365, 310, 270, 248, and 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> c). When the current density is decreased to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 60 cycles, the reversible capacity is recovered to 410 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Cycling performance is an important aspect for valuing the electrode materials of SIBs. FeS/CA with 68.6 % FeS (Figure S5) exhibits a high reversible specific capacity of 280 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> d). Furthermore, the CE of FeS/CA anode increases from about 57 % for the first cycle to quickly stabilize at approximately 99 % for the subsequent cycles, demonstrating the good reversibility (Figure S7). The TEM image of FeS/CA anode after 200 cycles (charged to 3.0 V) is shown Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> e. It reveals that the NPs with the size of about 5–10 nm are still distributed on the microporous carbon. The HRTEM image of show the fringes with a spacing of 0.293, corresponding to the (100) plane of FeS (Inset of Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> e), demonstrating the excellent stability of the microstructure. The outstanding performance of the FeS/CA stems from its unique aerogel structure. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie201610301-fig-0003">3</a> f, the mesopores in FeS/CA can store the electrolyte to shorten the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup> cations. The micropores act as ideal barrier to restrain the polysulfide anions dissolution to electrolyte during the discharge process, which can prevent the loss of active material. Both of them can synergistically enhance the rate performance and stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0026_R_d198112942e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Carbon">26</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the carbon framework of CA not only promotes rapid charge-transfer reaction but also improves the mechanical performance of the FeS nanoparticles, as well as buffers their volume changes, resulting in prolonged cyclic life-time of the electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie201610301-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201610301-bib-0013_R_d198112942e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the “double-helix” structure in a carrageenan–metal hydrogel was first used to create porous M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>/CAs (FeS/CA, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/CA, Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CA, CuS/CA, ZnS/CA, CdS/CA) with ultra-small, stabilized M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> NPs and hierarchical pore structure. This unique structure enhances the sodium-ion storage performance through promoting the electron/ion transport kinetics and stabilizing the structures of M<sub><i>x</i></sub>S<sub><i>y</i></sub>. The FeS/CA anode material exhibits a high reversible capacity of 380 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> as well as excellent cycling stability (280 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 200 cycles) and rate performance (222 mA h<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) for SIBs. This work provides a green and effective strategy for preparing multiple metal sulfides, since the sustainable seaweed biomass with double-helix structure not only leads to the formation of hierarchically porous framework, but also acts as nontoxic sulfur source.</p>
         
         <div class="article-section__content">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie201610301-sec-0002-title"> Acknowledgements</h3>
